<tjadc> Hello, how would I go about creating a process that can be controlled by
	a particular group ?  [16:20]
<grump> tjadc, what do you mean?
<tjadc> group here meaning process group
<tjadc> *
<tjadc> sorry not process group
<tjadc> a user group  [16:21]
<tjadc> I want to create a process that any member of a particular user group
	can control
<grump> What's the nature of this process?
<tjadc> kinda like file management.. but now for processes
<tjadc> The nature of the process is that it needs to be killed by any member of
	a particular group  [16:22]
<tjadc> whether it be running vi or X  [16:23]
<tjadc> it doesn't matter what the process is
<tjadc> I want it to be killed by a particular group, that's what matters.
<tjadc> Do you know whether this could be done ?  [16:24]
<grump> I'd create a kind of wrapper that any member of a particular group could
	ssh to (for example) to have the wrapper kill the process.
<grump> As far as I'm aware, "process management" of the sort you're talking
	about doesn't exist for linux.  [16:25]
<grump> Alternative to that might be some kind of tracking daemon that can (in
	your example) kill the process as root.  [16:26]
<tjadc> grump: Alright, thanks for your response :)  [16:27]

	   script.rb&

      itself.  search for deamonize. :)
<catphish> correct, there are ruby libraries designed to background ruby scripts
									  [21:16]
<catphish> which is the best way

      daemonize

<jelly-home> kwasd: you might minimize the resyncing time by adding the
	     write-intent bitmap feature to the md array  [07:38]

	     array at any time basically

	    anything serious

	    explicitly send traffic to that port though  [09:10]

<c3l> coachz: it copies all files. otherwise it wouldn't really be a backup ;)
      the purpose of a backup is essentially a snapshot of some files at some
      specific time. but if we only make hardlinks, future changes to the files
      will the propagate into the backups (since they are only hardlinks)

      be problematic if some file that is _not_ a hard link in the backup does

      \;   then /my/path/   will only contain files that do not have more than
      one hardlink  [22:41]

<c3l> coachz: Also, I found the rsync command we have been looking for:
      --compare-dest  [22:49]

<vipkilla> is it possible to copy replicate DNS from windows to linux? using
	   bind or powerdns on linux
<c3l> Assume directory A has some files, directory B has a subset of the files
      in A. You essentially want the complement of B with A as our
      universe. assuming you are familiar with mathematical language =) You want
      the files that are in A but NOT in B. so we call rsync --compare-dest=B A
      C. C contains what we want, since rsync does not copy files that are the
      same on source as dest.

									  [02:05]
<mbrownnyc> any assistance is appreciated
<eviljames> brucelee: Hardware devices need to be turned into a LVM Physical
	    Volume (pvcreate /dev/sda2 or whatever).  Once you have pvs, you
	    create groups of them, (vgcreate VolumeGroupName /dev/sda2 /dev/sdb2
	    ...)
<lautriv> brucelee, why a symlink, you have to mount it anyway ?
<eviljames> brucelee: Once you've got a volume group made, you create logical
	    volumes out of that.  (lvcreate -L 20g -n LVName VGName)
									  [02:06]
<eviljames> brucelee: Then you'll have entries like /dev/VGname/LVname - mount
	    away!
<eviljames> brucelee: man vgcreate ; man pvcreate ; man lvcreate ; man lvs ; man
	    vgs ; man pvs ; man lvextend (that one is particularly useful)
<eviljames> brucelee: have fun  [02:07]

	    run an abort instead maybe

<kessius> hi I'm newbie in linux,   I'm logged with user root #  - what key I
	  type to create the command ->   for i in $(seq  ); do ... done
									  [11:44]
<wildcard0> kessius: you can do it right there at #  [11:45]
<wildcard0> but be careful, you can hose the systems with loops and root
<[R]> kessius: "what key"?
<MouseTheLuckyDog> wildcard0, What do you mean by 'userspace"? Could root do it?
									  [11:50]
<wildcard0> ok so there's 2 sets of "space
<wildcard0> er
<wildcard0> "space" in linx  [11:51]
<wildcard0> linux
<wildcard0> damn i totally cant type tonight
<wildcard0> there's userspace, where you log in (including root) and run stuff.
	    it gets a pid, it runs, it shuts down, etc
<[R]> space... the FINAL frontier
<wildcard0> and then there's "kernel space".  that's where the kernel runs.  it
	    has its own memory, and it runs continuously  [11:52]
<wildcard0> there's no shell to kernel space (though that'd be kinda cool to
	    write)
<kessius> wildcard0, thank you
<wildcard0> basically, you need to either know C well or be very open to
	    learning and willing to invest a lot of time to get in there
									  [11:53]
<MouseTheLuckyDog> wildcard0, what I mean is can root create a process in kernel
		 space that could trap that call.
<wildcard0> no, you'd need to modify the source there
<wildcard0> the only ways to talk to kernel space are via the /proc and /dev and
	    /sys filesystems where you have things you can turn off and on
<MouseTheLuckyDog> wildcard0, I'm a fairly good C programmer.
<wildcard0> ok then you're off to a good start  [11:54]
<wildcard0> you may want to take a look at this:
<wildcard0> http://tldp.org/LDP/lkmpg/2.6/html/lkmpg.html
<wildcard0> and maybe this:
<wildcard0>
	    http://crashcourse.ca/introduction-linux-kernel-programming/introduction-linux-kernel-programming
<wildcard0> basically, you need to get some background in how this all fits
	    together  [11:55]
<wildcard0> after that, it's not too unreasonable to go hunting for specific
	    things and modify them
<wildcard0> it helps a lot to run stuff in a VM for testing so you dont hvae to
	    keep rebooting and your changes dont crash your box
<wildcard0> warning though: this is a fairly large set of knowledge and a big
	    project. assume you wont bet it done by the weekend :)  [11:56]
<wildcard0> i hope that helps.  it's not a straight up solution, but it's not a
	    simple problem.  [11:58]
